Title

Adrift on a Sleepless Sea

Carmen Ávila

Translated from Spanish into English by Olivia Caputo

Awarded the 2013 Rafael Ramírez Heredia National Story Prize by the Tampico Tamaulipas Council in Mexico.

Prologue

As a reader, I sometimes prefer to wait until the end of a book to read the prologue. I’m always worried it will spoil something important in the story. But now that I’m the one writing the prologue, I’m not remotely tempted to quote paragraphs from the book, or even to give you a general synopsis, merely an appetizer.

Adrift on a Sleepless Sea is divided, or united, as you’ll see, into three parts. In each of these parts, Carmen proves her narrative chops with her irreverent style. She’s a writer who takes risks with her different approaches, and the results are well worth it. She constructs for us a cathedral of absurdism, surrealism, and magical realism. She interweaves the detective novel with the anecdote and the fable into the shape of a lively narrative, unburdened by excesses, and infused with the sense of humor and naturalness that are her greatest weapons.

Carmen has created an atemporal dreamland that bends the laws of physics, where characters come and go, drifting through her stories. Adrift on a Sleepless Sea contains the reincarnated spirit of a twisted Scheherazade who climbed into bed with the Brothers Grimm as well as something of an exotic Robert Louis Stevenson that dreamed he was a butterfly that dreamed he was an exotic Robert Louis Stevenson.

Carmen Ávila’s narrative has one other specific advantage, which is discernible from the very first page: she is an excellent poet. It would suffice just to say she’s a poet, but to my mind she’s an excellent one. As a writer who is also able to navigate the waters of poetry, she can draw from wider range of resources to conjure the images she floats our way, rendering them very real in our minds, for all their unreality. 

The stories in this book are built upon the author’s influences, obsessions, and memories. She’ll explain them in her own time within these pages, so I see no reason to repeat them here. I will mention, however, that if Carmen were to spin a globe and stop it by pointing at random, it would not come as a shock if her finger landed on a place that she has been before. This is due to her insatiable curiosity to know other cultures, places, and people. When she sent me the manuscript for Adrift on a Sleepless Sea with an invitation to write this prologue, she gave as a reason (in addition to our shared affinity for literature and the friendship that unites us), our love of traveling the world. To be fair, there are some glaring differences between us; Carmen is less selfish when she travels (though all travelers are inherently somewhat selfish, which is not necessarily a bad thing). Carmen manages to get involved in humanitarian causes, undertake academic studies, participate in conferences, get under people’s skin, live simply when necessary, learn languages. All of this has made her more sensitive and empathetic, which leads to a more comprehensive understanding of those faraway places, an understanding for which she is repaid when the time comes to create her literary postcards.

To travel is to leave one’s comfort zone, to revert to being a child; a being that lacks the words to express oneself, for whom new foods are unfamiliar. To travel is to once again experience that forgotten fear and uncertainty. Carmen is an example of that perpetual return to childhood and its stories: in this case they are the myths and legends of the other adventurers we meet along the way.

Incidentally, her book reminds me of a phrase I once heard, by the songwriter (and avid traveler) Facundo Cabral, which goes something like this: “If the world is round, then I don’t know what it means to go forward.” Likewise, Carmen’s ship roams without a compass, guided by the swells and storms that bring in its stories. But I’ll stop myself here, so as not to give away any of those details that will ruin the surprise. 

Finally, I want to thank my dear, admired Carmen Ávila for allowing me to be a part of this book; I know how important it is to her. So without further delay, I invite you to board this rudderless ship of stories and undertake, as I have had the privilege to do, a marvelous journey through the author’s sleepless sea. 

––––––––

Miguel Barquiarena

The Shortest Story in All German Literature (1945)

The Shortest Story in All German Literature

––––––––

Ja!

(Siegfried Wolf, 1945)

Mariscal Sucre International Airport, Quito, Ecuador

Monday, May 23, 10:45am

Our airplane finally lands in Ecuador. No one is there to greet us as we navigate the international airport in Quito, the capital city of the “world’s belly button.” Andrés, Jade, and I barely manage to haul our heavy suitcases through the long corridor to go hail a taxi that will drop us off at the cheapest hotel we can find, since we’re low on cash. My prosthetic leg slows us down as I drag myself along. Once we’ve made it  through customs, Jade and I with freshly stamped passports, Andrés pulls out a bottle of tequila and takes a swig as we walk. What an asshole; doesn’t even offer us a sip.

On the Danube

A slightly insane, foreign collector placed an internet ad selling, for 20 euros, the historical remnants of the “Oberdonau Zeitung,” the premiere newspaper from the province in which Hitler was born. It primarily served to inform workers at the factory and nearby concentration camp of bad tidings from the war.

The newspaper had dwindled to only two sheets as the war dragged on. The second to last edition, from the 26th of April, 1945, declares that American tanks are entering the town and Russian soldiers (the Soviet army) have reached the forests on the outskirts of Vienna. Classified ads inquire about missing persons, in hopes of finding them alive, and about private property that was destroyed by the bombings.

Roberto Bolaño claimed that the final issue of “Oberdonau Zeitung” was printed on the 27th of April, 1945. It consisted of a single page, on which was published a famous story by German author Siegfried Wolf: the shortest story in all German literature.

The Hand of God and the Foot of D10S

Diego “El Pelusa” Maradona was always telling me about that blessed day, the 22nd of July 1986, when Argentina beat England in the world cup quarterfinals. The match was a matter of national pride because of the Falklands (A.K.A. Malvinas) War. With Mexico City’s Estadio Azteca completely packed, (a pause, for effect), Maradona, our “Barrilete Cósmico” – who earned almost as many nicknames as he did points – made the goal of the century. “The Golden Boy” our “D10S,” Number 10 scored! “Goooooooooooal!” screamed the crowd; even the Mexican spectators were swept away on a wave of Argentinean patriotism. The stadium, the country, all Latin America wept with joy. The victory was decided while the English players were still protesting, “Hand! Hand! Hand!” in the ref’s face. That was the same year Mexico lost to Germany in Monterrey, robbing us of the chance to be crowned champions on our home turf in our world cup. They had decided to hold the match in Monterrey because they knew that the southern heat and humidity would kill the Germans. But luck was against us because that day in Monterrey it was so cold that the Mexican players, most of whom were from the south of the country, nearly froze to death instead.

To this day, in the clinic, they’ll put on the video and listen to Victor Hugo Morales call 2-0, Argentina-England: "Passes to Diego: Maradona has it; two defenders on him; he moves the ball. One of the world’s greatest players; he breaks through on the right, past a third, and here comes Burruchaga for a pass! But it’s still Maradona... Yes! Yes! Go! Go-go-go-go-there... Gooooooalll!! Gooooooalll! Oh God, I could cry! Oh my God! This is football! Goal! Diegooooo! Maradooona! Forgive me, I can’t stop weeping. Maradona, in an unforgettable run! The play of the century; ‘barrilete cósmico,’ what planet did you come from? He just left the English players in the dust! The crowd is on its feet, and they’re all screaming for Argentina! Argentina two; England zero. Diegoal! Diegoal! Diego Armando Maradona! Thank God, for football, for Maradona, for these tears, for this... Argentina two; England zero." At the clinic, we’d all jump from our seats and laugh and cry tears of joy, while our hero, Diego Maradona, with that foul-mouth and barrel chest, grins at us from ear to ear.

Cayambe, Quito’s Red-Light District

Monday, May 23, 2:20pm

Once we check into our dump of a hotel in the neighborhood of Cayambe, which is also the Red-Light district, we head out to find some food. We end up at a nearby place serving local fare. From the very first mouthful Jade and I are already homesick for corn tortillas and hot sauce. We both start to cry. I think it’s partly due to nostalgia and partly because we might never find Siegfried Wolf, and then this entire trip will have been completely pointless.

Andrés has a few beers and starts spouting utter nonsense interspersed with Mexican pick-up lines. The waitress just stares at him, her eyes growing wide with fear. He tells her he’s Mexican, asks how much she charges per night, and gives her our hotel address and room number. He proposes the four of us have a “reverse gangband,” on top of implying that Jade and I are lesbians. Afterwards, we spend the rest of the afternoon and evening in Quito’s main square, lounging in front of the Cathedral, like the good little broke tourists we are.

The Shortest Story in All the World

Luis Gómez was my German lit teacher. He liked to brag about having written the shortest story in all the world. It was a single piece of paper on which one could read the following:

Now it would be easy to look at that and assume that a fly had taken a shit precisely on the center of the page, but no. His story was a period: a simple, solitary period. When it was published in the country’s most prestigious and serious literary magazine (which, I should mention, was a real feat as he had never published anything else and only managed this because the editor owed him a favor), it produced a chain reaction: all manner of accolades flooded the newspapers, independent reviews, and literary internet forums. But so, too, did a wave of sarcastic, biting criticism.

His detractors claimed that he was mocking his reader. They complained bitterly: “We live in an age where there is no respect nor ethics in the arts, and we see literature being written, more and more, by those utterly lacking in scruples.” His dearest friends showered him with flowers and flattery. They took it upon themselves to hunt down metaphors: “With this period, the writer symbolizes the end of literature: after this, nobody can write anything new anymore; it’s all been said, there’s nothing more to add.” 

Thanks to the story’s publication, Luis Gómez was offered countless honorary doctorates; he gave lectures in several universities in the United States, among them Stanford and Yale. But he also spoke at the most prestigious institutions in Europe like Oxford, Humboldt, Salamanca, La Sapienza, and the Sorbonne. Even though his specialty was technically German literature, the university decided to offer him a special professorship in “nanofiction,” as microfiction had already gone out of style: he himself had rendered it obsolete with his story.

Puyo, Pastaza Province

Tuesday, May 24, 5:45am (dawn)

We’re up early to buy our bus tickets to get to Puyo, a village in the Ecuadorian Amazon where, according to Roberto Bolaño, the famous author Siegfried Wolf was last spotted. We arrive in the afternoon. Andrés brings us to a house next to the main church and knocks on the door. A man comes out and, upon seeing Andrés, immediately embraces him. This is the parish priest, Father Plasencia.

Andrés grew up attending Catholic school, which is how he knows the priest, who immediately invites us into his home. I watch as Father Plasencia and Jade engage in some light flirtation. The priest ushers us in, indicating the dirt path leading to the patio in the house’s center. Andrés struggles to contain his laughter as he whispers to us: “this son of a bitch used to beat the students at the institute in Guayaquil, and now he has a kid here with a local Achuar woman.”

Rehab

My landlady used to come into my room and meticulously examine my possessions, or what few there were. My room was a shabby hole, which I never cleaned; the floor was covered in hair, lint, and a layer of accumulated dust. Her inventory of my belongings would have included the following items: pants, underwear, some old sneakers, a red athletic jacket from the former Soviet Union’s Olympic team (a gift from my brother), unfinished first drafts of novels, a bedspread that I’d thrown on the floor, a fan, some toilet paper, sanitary pads, an umbrella, some unopened makeup, a trash can full of crumpled up paper, and a dead beetle whose passing had affected me deeply. I had run into the street, tears in my eyes, crying: “I’ve killed Gregor Samsa!” As I mourned, my landlady hurled threats at me from the doorway.

In my room there was also a small lizard that (I later found out) was responsible for eating the insects who mistakenly entered, like cockroaches or the brown moths that habitually threw themselves against the buzzing lightbulb. One day I asked my landlady if I could borrow a broom and tried to sweep the lizard out of there. This caused the reptile’s tail to detach, though it continued to move independent of her body, which was now hiding in the doorframe. I shooed her out as best I could, but then I thought that she might suffer greatly in the world without her tail (like those women who suffer because they have no tits or ass), so I decided to hit her a few times with the broom. She just looked at me with her perfectly round, black eyes, which seemed to be requesting that I stop hitting her. Later I remembered having learned in high school biology that lizards can lose their tails and grow them back: it was too late by then. I had killed the one being that offered me any companionship, my good friend, who also took care of pest control. I threw her into the patio, half dead.  

Today I woke up with big red welts all over my body from the bedbugs in my mattress. 

Moving on with the inventory of my room, I had the following books, though there weren’t many and they technically belonged to the library: 

A Larousse Spanish-Spanish Dictionary

Nazi Literature in the Americas by Roberto Bolaño

Eva Braun and Adolf Hitler by Pere Bonnin

The Langenscheidt Dictionary of Modern German.

I used to shut myself up in that room and parade around in my underwear, admiring my lithe body and long hair in the wall-mounted mirror. In the mornings, I would water the marijuana plants I was growing in the balcony’s flower boxes. 

One day my landlady stumbled upon my merchandise (for a business I had only established to earn money to buy my ticket to Ecuador). For that matter, she couldn’t believe that I was just a thin, pretty girl; she assumed that I must also be moonlighting as a prostitute and exotic dancer. When she found the syringes, she decided to alert my parents. They put me on a plane to Cuba, where I was checked into rehab.

I Later Found Out

Before heading to Spain to pursue his special post doctorate in nanofiction, Luis (now no longer Doctor Gómez to me; I had decidedly crossed the student-teacher line when we became lovers), introduced me to one of his students: Andrés Yañez. He was Ecuadorian, and had come to Mexico to study literature for the sole purpose of becoming the greatest poet in Ecuadorian history; though he was also contemplating a return to his country to go into politics as a tyrant, using hunger and fear to control the Ecuadorian population for the several-decade duration of his government. But that’s another story.

From the moment Andrés and I locked eyes, I pitied him. His pupils had the same glimmer as a stray dog just begging you to adopt it because it’s gone a few too many days without eating. In fact, Andrés had spent the better part of his life emulating Bukowski (his literary hero, whom he admired above all things, and whom he was constantly quoting): this meant that he divided his time between libraries, prostitutes, and alcohol. From the moment Luis introduced us, I knew that Andrés was going to fall in love with me, but that Luis would be an obstacle; I really did love him, and Andrés had a great respect for him.

That night we went out to a gay bar. Andrés spotted a trans woman with enormous silicon breasts: “How much to show me your tits?” he asked her. She replied, “three hundred, room included.” But Andrés didn’t have any money. His income came from what little his father, a retired colonel, was able to send him and he spent it in clubs or cheap, dirty rooms when he found someone to spend the night with. The list of women he’d been with spanned all races and continents.

When I proposed my thesis to Luis, he sent me a Roberto Bolaño essay, which hadn’t been included in the book Nazi Literature in the Americas; the editors had taken it out because a stranger had shown up at the publisher’s offices and paid to interrupt the printing and destroy, before their very eyes, this particular essay, which was part of the original manuscript. However, Bolaño, as he was dying from liver disease, not only worked tirelessly on his novel 2666, he also took the time to rewrite this essay, including it in his will because he knew how important it was. Luis had received it from an ex-girlfriend who lived in Barcelona, and she, in turn, had managed to get a copy of it through a Chilean friend of a friend of an acquaintance of the mother of a girl from down the block who was sleeping with the lawyer of the family who owned the campground where Bolaño worked as a night watchman. Luis had a strange obsession with the German writer who, according to Bolaño’s essay, had written, in the late stages of the Second World War, that enigmatic short story, published in the cultural section of the Oberdonau newspaper on the 27th of April in that fateful year: 1945.

Andrés was necessary to our plan because Bolaño wrote that the German author, Siegfried Wolf, had last been seen in Ecuador, somewhere in the Amazon rainforest. Jade and I hoped first to find Siegfried alive, and then to interview him, to at last decipher the enigma of his mysterious story.   

For that we needed someone capable of guiding us on a trek through the heart of the Ecuadorian Amazon to uncover Siegfried’s whereabouts. I didn’t find Andrés entirely reliable; for such a risky undertaking we needed a guide who was competent, to be sure, but, more importantly, we needed a guide who was sober. Still, despite being the son of an ex-military commander who had led an endless string of coups in Ecuador (then took up arms nine hundred fifty seven times and faced defeat just as many), Andrés had been a good boy scout, so maybe that would help us. Then I introduced him to Jade; I stood by as they gazed at each other for what felt like a long time, completely ignoring me. I knew that Andrés’s horny little heart had succumbed to my friend’s charms. Jade, with that ridiculous name of hers, like an Aztec stone. I later found out the rest: that they had indeed fucked each other. They each confessed to me separately; he told me: “Your friend has love handles, you can’t tell, because of that super tight belt she wears.” She told me: “He’s pretty small, you can’t tell, because he stuffs a folded sock inside his briefs.”

Somewhere in the Ecuadorian Rainforest

Wednesday, May 25, 7:00am

Several armed men arrive at the parish. Among them is the famous guerilla fighter Andrés Sarquis, renowned throughout the Amazon; his camp must be nearby. Everyone in the village knows how to get in touch with him. The villagers gather provisions for the rebels, who offer protection in exchange for the food the people give them: a lovely little guerilla ecosystem. We’re fascinated by how the vestiges of the largely extinct, rural guerrilla forces that Régis Debray devoted so much time and attention to – an interesting parallel since it’s his fault that Che Guevara was discovered hiding in Bolivia – remain active in the Ecuadorian wilderness.

Strange as it seems, I note a strong resemblance between our Andrés and General Andrés Sarquis. They’re almost carbon copies of each other, except that General Sarquis is graying and looks worn out from his time in the mountains. Our Andrés Yañez, 23 years Sarquis’s junior, is also worn out, but by alcohol, whoring, and the hunger he experienced in Mexico. I swear they look like father and son.

The general is cordial towards us, inviting us to visit their camp. Once there, we observe some combat-training maneuvers drawn up by the men. We get to see how they exercise and cook their food. They store just enough water to drink and shave, which means they bathe in a nearby stream. I swear that Sarquis and Andrés have the same facial expressions and even make the same, odd gestures. I think their tone of voice is almost identical, too. It’s honestly a surprise they don’t share a last name as well as the first.

Andrés fills the general in on our mission to find a famous writer who, after the Second World War, fled Germany to seek refuge in Ecuador. General Sarquis is pleased to discover that we’re literature students; he shows us his books, which he reads in his downtime. Among them are the diaries of Che Guevara and Mao’s Red Book. The general reads us some of the poems he’s written throughout the years. Jade starts crying and applauds him, blubbering that his verses are beautiful. To stave off depression, she asks for a glass of water so she can take her Prozac. Then the general notices my right leg is missing, and covertly asks Andrés what happened to me. Andrés answers him, “she lost it in combat.” The general regards me appreciatively, probably supposing me to be an ex-guerrilla; he starts trying to connect with me over the guerrilla lifestyle, about which I’m clueless. Of course the poor man couldn’t know that I’m an addict, and I lost my leg after injecting drugs into it when I couldn’t find a vein in my arms anymore.

Sarquis tells us about a remote estate in the mountains, where an old widower lives with his maid. At first the rebels tried to attack the mansion, but, while old-fashioned in appearance, it had a surprisingly advanced security system, probably imported from Germany. In the first failed attack General Sarquis lost two men to electrocution, and while others did manage to enter the garden by digging a tunnel, they were met by three German shepherds who left them without noses or ears. General Sarquis then surrounded the house with some more of his men, but they were met with grenade launchers and machine gun fire. The maid deftly handled the grenade launcher while the old man shot at Sarquis’s men from his wheelchair any time they managed to get close to the door. Faced with this onslaught, Sarquis’s men decided to flee, so there were no further deaths to mourn. In the end, the general announced his surrender, simply requesting food for his lean and miserable troops. The old man barked something at the maid, who relayed the message to Sarquis: “My patron says all he wanted was to be left alone. He’s a World War II veteran; he’s only helping you because he respects soldiers.”

General Sarquis was grateful for the sacks of flour that the old gentlemen gave them. “How should I address my benefactor?” he had asked.

“Call him Herr Wolf” answered the maid before slamming the door.

“If you plan to have dealings with that man, you’ll need to tread carefully. This Hairwolf guy is a crazy old Kraut,” he advised us. But we were confident that Siegfried Wolf wouldn’t deny us a simple interview. After all, we only wanted to decipher the enigma of his story published in Oberdonau in 1945 and, if there was time, ask if he’d kept a copy of it that he could sign for us.

At this point, the guerrillas started to carouse and drink. They ended up drinking enough to render them useless in any possible standoff we might have with Siegfried and his maid the following day.

Meeting D10S

In Cuba, I met Diego Maradona, D10S Himself. When I got to the rehab clinic, I immediately asked about Him. Most of His cohort was made up of His Argentine compatriots and local, Cuban admirers; they told me not to bother Him, that D10S was sick of signing autographs. I pleaded with them and they told me, “alright then, che, but only for a second, got it?”

I found D10S watching the knockout stage of the Euro Cup and cutting cocaine. “You new here?” He asked without glancing up at me. I couldn’t believe it. D10S had addressed me; I was speechless. He turned to the orderly, “Martín, treat esta pibe well, okay?” D10S had actually asked His cronies to take care of me; He probably took to me because I’m Mexican. The thought died as my gaze landed on His hand, that blessed hand, the famous hand that had defeated the English in 1986. And I wanted to kiss it, because that’s what you did when you got close to D10S. You knelt before Him and paid Him homage. You kissed that hand in deference, as if it were Don Corleone before you. 

And so began my close friendship with D10S. I was by His side both day and night, and, on several occasions, kept Him from killing Himself; like that time He climbed up on the facility’s boundary wall and threatened to throw Himself off of it, even though everyone was begging Him to come down as international news cameras stood by, eager to cover the story of a god’s literal fall. He balanced on one foot and everyone shouted “nooooooo!” He balanced on the other and everyone shouted “oooohhh!” as if He were a performer in some drunken ballet: “Let’s go Dieguito, stop playing, Wacho” (by then I’d been talking with Him so much, I’d started to adopt His lunfardo slang) “we’re not at the world cup final, why all the drama, che?”

Best in the World

Miniature Amazonian Neuschwanstein

The doorbell is positioned so that you have to reach high up to ring it, and we realize that, without meaning to, we’re giving the photo a Nazi salute. Through the intercom a voice, old-sounding and with a thick German accent, demands to know we want. That’s when he opens the security gate to allow us pass into the garden.

To get from the garden entrance to the mansion door, we have to travel about six hundred feet. Across that distance, we see an old man in a wheelchair, petting his three German shepherds. He tells the dogs to “Steh” when they see us and ready themselves to run and attack us: they freeze at his command. The elderly man approaches in his electric wheelchair. The chair’s motor is fast. It only takes him a few seconds to arrive in front of us. He raises a hand to greet us. He’s wearing a brown shirt to which are pinned several war medals. Jade giggles and murmurs to me that his little mustache reminds her of Charlie Chaplin. I keep my thoughts to myself for now, but I get the feeling that we’ve uncovered something the world meant to keep buried.

Welcome to Little-Jump, (A.K.A. Saltillo)

(Some notes about me, my family, and my home)

Papa is a Doll Made of Old Rags

When the S.S. officials entered our house, I had no trouble recognizing them thanks to the swastikas on their sleeves. My father and I were in the central patio at the time. Before I climbed onto the roof to try to escape, my father handed me a photograph. “This is the key,” he told me. From my perch, I watched as the soldiers barged in and grabbed him by his shoulders. Hanging by his armpits as they dragged him away, Papa looked like a doll made of old rags and scraps. The toes of his dilapidated shoes scraped along the ground. The fingertips of his gloves had been eaten away by moths and he wore an old, Russian ushanka, and a dirty coat that was patched at the elbows. He offered no resistance as they carried him off, their military-issue helmets gleaming. I ran away along the roof so they wouldn’t see me. The photograph he’d given me was blurry, and no matter how long I looked at it, I couldn’t make out the image, only a mass of meaningless colors. Nor did I know what he meant by calling it “the key,” but I kept the photo anyway.

Jesus is Angry Because I Ate his Cloud

The sky was full of clouds that day; but it looked different somehow. When I looked up as I went out to the patio to play, the sky’s appearance made me run right back inside. I was hungry. So hungry, in fact, that I thought the clouds were made of cotton candy, which my mom bought for me whenever we went out. I had only to bite the pink and spongy sweet for it to hit my tongue and instantly melt in my mouth. I thought that eating a cloud would be a similar sensation. I went outside again and started throwing stones at the sky, trying to knock down a cloud to calm my aching belly. The first stone I threw didn’t reach the sky. I threw another with the same result: it fell right back down, landing at my feet. I threw the third, and this finally knocked down a piece of cloud to eat. I shoved the cloud into my waiting mouth, but, much to my disappointment, it just tasted like water. I looked up to find that the piece of cloud that I’d eaten had left a hole in the sky. Jesus Christ leaned out of the hole I’d made in the cloud. In the middle of his chest he had a small, flaming heart surrounded by thorns, as if it were a pin to hold up his robes. He didn’t say anything; he only glared at me. I knew that God was angry with me because I ate a piece of his property, and I felt very bad and ashamed. I felt so bad that I ran back into my grandmother’s house. When I dared a glance outside to see if Jesus had gone away, I saw that he was still there, remaining completely silent. But he kept looking at me in that ominous, angry way.

Ice Skating Prostitutes

The brothel I work at has an ice-skating rink. We serve food and drinks on skates, and all the girls have to put on those pretty costumes to make us look like real figure skaters. The stretchy material has the added benefit of allowing for flexibility. I go to my room to change into my costume. I’m not wearing any underwear, so I get completely undressed. My boss calls me in and tells me off, threatening violence: “how can you make the clients wait?” she demands. As she leaves, she orders me to see to the patrons. That’s when I finally decide, “never again. Never again,” and I get the hell out of there. I steal a client’s  motorcycle to make my escape. “Never again,” I repeat to myself, the cold air buffeting my shoulders as I drive down the lonely highway away from the city. I glance up at the night sky. The white and gray clouds on the horizon tell me that rain’s not far off.

Why I’m Single

I was at a party. Everyone was laughing because my boyfriend was sharing amusing anecdotes, but then he told one that was about me. I couldn’t hear him well, but everyone else laughed when he got to the end. I grabbed a rifle from a nearby soldier, pointed it at my boyfriend, and muttered “fucking Nazi” as I pulled the trigger, emboldened by the fact that the asshole had been making jokes at my expense.

He lay stretched out on the floor, his green military uniform, with the armband bearing its leader’s insignia, stained with blood. I stood there, stewing in my anger, while the other guests stared at me in perplexed silence.

The End of the World

An orange-colored ball of light appears in the middle of the sky, announcing the end of the world. 

I’m in a clinic with mostly older people (my landlords among them). They’re staring up from their armchairs to look at the sun’s position. Of course it’s not really the sun. I’m peering through the screen on my window. Everywhere, the sky is opening in the same way. I call my dad on my cell phone. I tell him it’s the end of the world, and please come to Monterrey-Vietnam to get me.

I’m not sure if that bright, orange stain, which could easily be mistaken for the sun (though I know it’s not), is perhaps one (or several) meteorites hurtling towards the earth, or if it’s maybe the second coming of Jesus. But I know for certain it’s the end of the world. My father answers the phone with an “aaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaaah!” and laughs. I tell him to “fuck off” and hang up on him: I’m not in the mood for jokes, it’s the end of the goddamn world.

Shortly afterwards my brother arrives with the rest my family. They came as soon as they could, driving from Little-Jump to Monterrey-Vietnam, all the while circumventing the perils of the highway with its fog and pollution. Their arrival lifts my mood; at least the end of the world won’t be boring.

The Fence

My brother and I were returning from Little-Jump-Israel, where we went to a school to study the Torah and Hebrew. My brother had glasses with black acetate frames and wore a dark blue yarmulke. I had been with the other women who were also learning to read the Talmud. When we got home, we happened upon a thug attempting to hop over the fence and break in. My brother defended us to the best of his ability, which is to say he picked up a wooden pallet and chucked it over the fence to deter our would-be assailant.

Papa is a Broken Sieve

Years later, I went back to visit the primary school I attended as a child. It still had the same gardens, the same brick walls, painted canary yellow on the outside and cream for the inner walls, interrupted by the green of the chalkboards. I dropped in on one of the classes: the children were learning their ABCs. The dismissal bell rang, and the kids scattered before it like a flock of birds being shot at by an amateur hunter.

My father and brother came to pick me up at the school, but some black vans were parked outside when they arrived. The vans belonged to the opposition, and their riders got out and started shooting at us. My brother and my father took cover from the bullets behind the classrooms, but kept leaning out to return fire. I pulled out my pistol and started firing, too, but I missed, hitting my father instead. The bullet passed right through his heart. I let out a panicked scream that seemed to go on forever. I’d never told him how much I loved him. My father was nothing more than air. I tried in vain to trap it with my fists.

De Gourmet

I watch from the second-floor window as several tanks amble down the street while soldiers raise their rifles.

We’d made it to this house along with others who’d fled, seeking food and shelter. The house perhaps once belonged to a wealthy family. It’s beautiful, and I have a vague recollection of having seen the family descend the front steps to climb into their black limousine, trailing several blond children. Either that or I know them from having worked as their servant long ago.

We’re out of food. I sneak another look out onto the street. I don’t have a mirror to confirm whether the hunger is visible in my sunken eyes.

It’s dangerous outside; nobody wants to leave for fear of being shot. I don’t know where my brother and mother are. Maybe in the buildings out front, but I can’t go looking for them. I don’t know when this war is going to end. I don’t even know why we’re at war. The only thing that matters is hunting rats because we’re already sick of eating cats and carrion.

Vertigo

My mother ended up inheriting a mansion located near San Luis Potosí-Dubai. I brought my mother to Shanghai-Little-Jump for a visit. We were starving, so we stopped into a shop to grab something to eat. The few Chinese classes I had taken in college didn’t help me here. I asked the shop assistant how much I owed him (this time in English), and he named a price that didn’t seem like nearly enough. My mother and I contentedly opened the can, asked for two plates, and sat down to eat our beans at a table next to the shop-restaurant in Little-Jump’s Chinatown.

Alcatraz

I, along with several others, had been kidnapped by a total psycho. He bound our hands and tied us to chairs. We had to watch while he sharpened his knives, occasionally glancing over at us to smile. A Skeleton in the Bed

In the Little-Jump cemetery, famous for its grand mausoleums, imported from Italy, there’s a tomb containing the remains of my three grandmothers.

World Cup Final

To avoid clashing, I also wore white, a suit with a skirt and jacket.
