I knew my brother had killed someone, because he came in with blood on his hands. When I saw that, it finally confirmed something I’d long feared about him. I knew he’d offed someone because, as he came inside, he was pulling a gun out of his waistband and smiling. I’d always known he was bloodthirsty; every weekend he made me watch BBC documentaries about the horrors the Nazis and Japanese committed during the war. He’d kiss me on the cheek and say “come on, sis. You don’t want to watch with me?” Then I had to watch because he’d get mad if I didn’t, which was the last thing I wanted. But after watching them I would have nightmares where the SS was following me or where I shot my  fiancé who was a Nazi.

Our mother claims my brother was born that way, bloodthirsty, because she went to see a movie about a soulless Roman general when she was pregnant. She was deeply affected by the film, and that energy must have flowed directly to the fetus that was my brother at the time, which turned him into a reprobate.

Whenever my brother arrives home, birds fly away and dogs run out of the room, heads bowed and tails between their legs. Blanco was the only dog who had ever dared to bite him, and when he realized his terrible mistake, he started trembling. He knew what was coming for him. My brother didn’t say anything, because he loved that dog, but a month later all the dog’s teeth fell out, one by one. He ended up completely toothless, as if by some magic, just for making the mistake of biting my brother, who chuckled: “I always say that if you try to come for me, you pay.”

That day when he got home, I knew my brother had murdered someone. I knew it because he was smiling when he came in.

Postmodern Prometheus

I was in his castle, face to face with the man himself. So this was the infamous doctor? The one who experimented to find a way to collect dead body parts and join them together to give them life. The castle was gloomy and poorly lit, a gothic structure constructed in black stone. He was standing there in front of me, looking every inch the aristocrat he was with his black hair hanging to his shoulders, dressed in a red silk robe and black cape. His servant had been bringing him the  body parts he needed for his experiments. The doctor took a sword, some kind of saber, and asked the servant to stand still in front of him. He told me: “now you’ll see how easily I can completely dismember a man. I’m quite deft in the art of breaking a man down and building him back up again.” With that, he made a series of precise swipes, his movements smooth and continuous. Swifter than a ray of light, he’d cut off the servant’s head, arms, and legs, all while the man’s stunned and fearful expression remained on his face. I was showered with blood, as was the doctor; body parts lay strewn on the floor. He didn’t bother to collect them. “That can wait,” he told me.

That done, he invited me to take a turn in his carriage. He got completely naked and instructed me to attach the horse’s harness to his body. He pulled the carriage, bouncing along like a self-satisfied rabbit, showing off his youthful physique. He pulled the coach by the bridle around his neck, his muscular body full of red blood as he pranced through the white landscape. The light of the full moon bled onto the path, illuminating the snow. As he drew me along in the carriage, I occasionally cracked the whip to make him go faster.

World Cup Final

The wedding was held in a restaurant with white tablecloths upon tables that held cakes with equally white frosting. They were delicious. To avoid clashing, I also wore white, a suit with a skirt and jacket. The restaurant building resembled old Moorish constructions brought to Spain by the Arabs and then to us, in the Americas, by the Spanish: there were rooms all around a central open space, but instead of having a fountain or tree in the middle, there was a soccer field.

I walked towards it, stopping at midfield, where the Mexican National team assembled around me to take the official photo. We were at a FIFA match. Specifically the World Cup Final against Germany, a team that we had thus far failed to defeat. We’d barely lined up on the field when the Mexican players started running towards the opposing team’s net and scored five goals within the first minute (taking  advantage of the fact that the Germans were not yet in position).

It quickly became clear who would win the cup, but the Germans rushed to defend their territory, and returned three goals in the second minute. As they fought over the ball, the players kept kicking it furiously and it flew through the air with great speed. One player head-butted the ball to get it out of dangerous territory, and the ball split open; the outer layer of cloth ripped apart and slid off revealing a woman with long hair, wearing a dress in the exact style of Empress Josephine of France. She took off running, and the rest of the players ripped off their uniforms and ran after her with their floor-length dresses, also from the Romantic period, flowing behind them.

“It’s not fair!” I yelled at the referee. “She’s a woman. FIFA holds a separate world cup for them! Why are we mixing men and women in this final?” Of course I didn’t stop to consider that I was also a woman in a FIFA world cup final, where Mexico beat Germany 5 to 3.

The Head Cutter

The following day, I discovered why I’d dreamed that my brother had killed someone. It was because he’d actually done it. He trudged in with the head of one José Ángel in hand, and my mother didn’t say a word about it. He threw it onto the floor at my feet: “Here. Bury it in the garden.”

“Where exactly?” I asked.

“Doesn’t matter,” he replied. I knew that if I started to dig a hole, the neighbors would see me, so I put up a screen before getting to work. But as soon as the neighbors heard the sounds of the shovel that accompanied my labor, they peered out to see what exactly I was burying.

“Why not take it to an empty lot and dump it there?” My mother chimed in.

But I cautioned, “what if someone sees? Better to throw it in a ditch.”

“No. We have to bury it. Keep digging, while mom gets me something to eat,” replied my brother. I threw myself into my work to get it over with. Now I knew why I had dreamed only yesterday that my brother had killed someone. The dream had actually come true.

The Bodies

They discovered two bodies. Two. We realized this because there were two white sheets, one covering each corpse. That’s what we saw: two white sheets, a green lawn, trees, and the river nearby. But we had only killed one person, so we weren’t sure who had taken advantage of our crime to commit another and leave the body there.

When the police detained him, we thought he would snitch on us; after all, we were his accomplices, but, miraculously, he didn’t say a thing. He only turned to glare at us while the police cuffed him and carried him off. When we went to collect our earnings for the job, we didn’t have a money counting machine on us. Jaime called for his cousin, who was blind. The blind cousin carefully examined the paper with his fingers and found several fake bills. He tore them in half while we looked on.

Desert Comics

In a lifeless desert, where not even airplanes flew overheard, somebody opened a comic-book store run by the pope himself. When I went there, I didn’t actually end up buying any comics, but our conversation turned to the possible succession of the Spanish throne.

A boy approached us and started speaking to me in Aramaic. I didn’t understand him, but then he mooned me, revealing his hairy ass, and it hit me that this was the devil incarnate. That’s when I learned of the dangers that can come from conversing with a saint.

Exploding Wheat

Even though I knew my friend was gay and didn’t really like women, I asked him to take my virginity. He enthusiastically agreed and even got a bit into it, I could tell from the way he kissed me.

There was another man who lived with us. I don’t know why, but he was always talking about how much he loved his wife. He’d say it while fixing his tie in the mirror and he’d call her on the phone just to tell her how much he loved her. No matter how often I flirted with him and hinted that I wanted to sleep with him, he paid me no mind and my attempts to seduce him went unanswered.

I think my friend only agreed to take my virginity because it was my final request. I was about to die. I had eaten that bread made of genetically modified wheat, and it contaminated my entire body. It was incurable. I reminded myself of this with a stoic acceptance of my martyrdom, as I boarded the train and my gay friend smiled at me.

My brother, mother, and father (in his wheelchair) had come to visit me in Monterrey-Bangkok, but they weren’t sad. At least my fate was preferable to that of the birds. When they ate the contaminated wheat, their heads exploded.

On Fornication

Of all the hookups I can recall, that time I fucked Condoleezza Rice after a Harvard graduation ceremony – even though a powerful black woman in North American politics with full lips and a red pantsuit and the fact that the act occurred at the culmination of the conferring of doctoral degrees, presented exclusively to women wouldn’t ordinarily have struck me as the sexiest thing in the world – wasn’t the one that intrigued me the most. The one that intrigued me the most was the time I fucked the world’s greatest soccer star, Maradona Himself.

We were at His place, I think, when I gently placed His right hand between my legs. This was the same hand, the blessed hand, with which He defeated England in the 1986 World Cup. What followed left me reeling for several days.

Pizza Delivery

To free ourselves from that bald psychopath, we came together, all of us, to kill him, slice him up, and arrange the pieces of him like pepperoni on a pizza, which we naturally proceeded to throw into the street. But the crust was so thin that it went flying like a frisbee towards the neighbors’ house.

When our neighbors saw it, they scarfed it down is a quickly as they could. It left dark red crumbs on their face. Visibly sick, with their disheveled hair, they looked almost like victims of the atomic bomb in Hiroshima. They ended up being violently ill with food-poisoning, all because of us.

The American and his Wife

We only had one factory left to inform of our community service project, which we had to complete as a graduation requirement. It was in a sketchy neighborhood: dirt roads, lots of repair shops and small businesses that made low-quality, low-cost parts to supply to foreign factories in the city. There were beggars on each corner getting high on glue or drunk on beer. In addition to the addicts, the neighborhood was also home to many drug dealers. Where one goes, the other follows. The residents’ houses were made of cardboard covered with greasy boards and metal sheets instead of concrete roofs. The houses didn’t have cement floors either, just dirt. The neighborhood was known as “Cardboardville.”

There was no electric lighting. House fires were frequent because the cardboard walls went up in flames easily when the inhabitants left candles burning either as a source of light or for the constant vigils held for the Virgin of Guadalupe or Jude the Apostle to pray for the impossible, be it work, money, love, the return of a baby-daddy, or a cure for disease. The children were thin, grimy, and unkempt as they ran around, only the luckiest among them even wearing underwear. Their hair was a nest for lice, the nits drinking up what little blood remained in their heads. Almost none of them attended school. The children had been hardened by an excess of sun, their hair so dry and neglected that the tips were starting to turn blond. Their stomachs were distended with parasites and worms. Their cheeks were burnt red from the wind, which had dried them out over the years, turning their little faces into a desert of chapped earth.

The real landscape wasn’t much better. Everything was dirt, including the dried-up stream; nothing grew in that place, not even a single weed. We’d heard there was a brothel nearby, which we never saw, but it was said that when the children walked to the secondary school (which only had three classrooms) they found naked women dumped on the path. They weren’t dead, likely only drugged. Worse were the cases of young girls’ being raped when they were forced to cross the barren terrain to get to the school.

We had to meet with the factory owners to introduce our project. We were going into the community to have a series of lectures, with which we hoped to help the adults break free from their ignorance and the children from their malnourishment, which could prevent them from growing, or even kill the less fortunate. Those it didn’t kill, it left as sickly adults. An old woman caught our attention when she started to yell at us from a distance: “get out of here, go away!” We thought she was a little nuts, or else was warning us about danger from the beggars. A junkie had stabbed a student twelve times over by the stream. The student bled out through his wounds, expiring just as the ambulance arrived. Unlike the old woman, everyone else welcomed us, believing us to be a political party. Just another group who would promise things in exchange for votes, and then leave, never to return. In their wake, they would only leave a free t-shirt, some lunch, and a coca cola. They thought that we were going to solve all their problems, from the lighting and dirt floors to the lack of running water and indoor plumbing.

We’d notified everyone else and were only missing this one workshop. They made jars and crockery and decorative pottery to then export to the United States. We knocked on the door, which was immediately pulled open by the owner, whom we noticed was American because of his white, though sunburnt, skin and his intense, blue gaze. He invited us in, flexing his impeccable Spanish. The shop was in a state of absolute disarray. Dead leaves had blown off the tree that was planted in the patio, and come in through the open, sliding glass door. The dry leaves, seeking out a bit of shade as the wind propelled them, were left scattered across the floor. A clay sun and moon hung on the wall, a sample of the pieces on offer there. The American lived in the house that was attached to the workshop; he was the manager. With him lived a young woman, who was gorgeous and tall with long, black hair. She watched him with tenderness and wouldn’t stop calling him “my love.”

We sat around the table, and he asked us about the project we’d come to propose: a skills training course for his employees. The woman, who was standing nearby, started to laugh uproariously. The American, not paying her any mind, kept talking to us, expressing his earnest interest in the project. He asked if he could financially help us to bring it about. The woman approached the table and, out of nowhere, started screaming that he was a killer, that he’d killed his wife. The American rose from his seat and walked over to a nearby chest of drawers, opened one of them, and pulled something out. The woman continued to scream, denouncing him for his crime. The American brought over a syringe, grabbed the woman’s arm, and injected something into it. The woman went still as she slipped into a stupor.

The American pensively straightened up and walked out to the patio. He stared long and hard at the tree. The wind started to blow harder. The red and yellow leaves of the tree began to fall. Suddenly a small green lake sprang from the earth, swallowing up the tree and flooding the house, drenching all its festering images, which had already polluted our minds.

Fiji Water

We passed by a river, which had been soiled by all the trash floating in it. I didn’t remember there being so much trash on the island; this water couldn’t be potable, surely. I went to the beach and met a tourist, whom I guessed was also Mexican. We started chatting. I told him I thought Cancún was more scenic than Fiji; the water was more crystal-clear in our home country. But still, the blue of Fiji’s waters was both deep and transparent. The man agreed with me. Then, he proceeded to perform a dive worthy of an Olympic competitor. He leapt backwards from the cliff we were on, which was very high, and landed perfectly, sliding right into the water. Another boy tried to imitate him, but he ended up hitting his head on a rock and just lay there, floating. The media arrived shortly thereafter. A reporter in a bathing suit stood near the corpse in the water, informing viewers of the regrettable occurrence while a camera filmed her from the shore.

On the island there were elephants, who were the sole proprietors of the only freshwater lake available: they furiously fought for ownership amongst themselves by slamming their tusks into the liquid. They spared me a glance and carried on fighting. The island’s inhabitants decided to try the elephants in court; it wasn’t fair that they were unwilling to share the water with the humans.

On the lakeshore, they improvised a courtroom by setting up some chairs in which were seated the twelve members of the jury and a judge; everyone was dressed in Thai military uniforms. They began proceedings against the pachyderms. The animals didn’t like this, and one of them charged towards the court on his thick legs. When the attendees saw the elephant coming, they all ducked except for the judge, who plowed ahead with his speech. The elephant wrapped his trunk around the judge’s waist and used it to launch him through the air. The water was theirs, and nobody was going to take it from them.

The few foreigners who lived on the island, which was mostly forested, had tried searching for water, digging wells all over. One of them had finally struck gold (or water), but in a negligible quantity; it wouldn’t even last the population a month. A German expat’s daughter had died from dehydration, a fate which he accepted with resignation. We attended her funeral. The girl’s father was dressed in white and chain smoking throughout. His wife informed him that there was no water left. For the last month the family of five had stretched themselves to survive on less than a quarter-cup of water each.

Shipwreck

As the captain of the ship, I gave the order to open fire. I knew it was a suicide mission. Still, if luck looked favorably upon us, we might manage to come out of it with a shipwreck. The brave soldiers rallied with their crossbows and cannons.

Once the enemy had reduced our ship to mere splinters, my black steed and I swam towards the floating wooden debris, which had started to swell with water. We clung to the makeshift raft, bored and melancholy, rocking in the waves. Perhaps someone, from the other side of sleep, would one day think to come and rescue us.

Adrift on a Sleepless Sea

The Parable of the Three Hermits

No one could quite figure out how old the Venerable Somsak Piyaseelo was. It seemed like he’d surpassed the Buddha himself from so much meditation. He might truly have overcome death, age, and illness, whereas Buddha had eventually succumbed to food poisoning from some undercooked meat (the sutras claim that it was pork).

The Venerable Somsak told us he meditated twice a day, “once when I open my eyes, and again when I close them.” Our calculations and conjectures about his age bewildered us. One night, he told us that as a young boy he would accompany his mother to the temple, where he met the Venerable Father Phramonkolthepmuni, but Phramonkolthepmuni had already been dead for over a hundred years. He spoke of the wise abbot as having a profound and unsettling gaze, but remembered him also as a man of infinite compassion, who could read the innermost thoughts of the human mind. He related how, when a cobra accidentally slithered into the temple one day, the Venerable Father simply looked at it, and the animal scurried away in fear.

The Venerable Somsak Piyaseelo was headed to South Africa because he had his small temple in Cape Town. If we ever wanted to find diamonds (he advised those of us aboard), we should look for them by digging through ostrich poop, because the birds favored the raw, precious stones as a food source.

We were surprised by his appearance that night, but he had heard our constant chatter, and come to reprimand us: “In the temple of Wak Paknam where I was ordained and spent most of my life, there were up to one hundred monks living in one small room. Nobody ever said a word. It wasn’t that we were angry, it’s just that one can survive silence; nobody ever died from not talking.” I think he told us this hoping that we’d all take the hint and shut up. We simply shared a look and carried on with our conversation. That’s when he told us the parable of the three hermits.

“Three hermits lived together in a cave. They’d gone years without speaking either amongst themselves or to anyone else, and they never went down to the village. One day, a lost bay horse wandered into the cave. A year later, one of the hermits said, ‘what a lovely pony.’

“The following year the next hermit answered, ‘I liked its shiny, jet black coat.’

“The following year the last of the hermits, in a tone both exhausted and annoyed, added, ‘if you’re going to keep arguing about the white elephant that wandered into the cave, I’m leaving this place today, and you’ll never see me again.’”

That shut us up for several hours, but only because we didn’t understand the parable despite all our head scratching. We examined the story from all angles, gnashing our teeth in frustration. Occasionally someone lifted an index finger as if about to ask a clarifying question, but immediately stopped themselves (afraid to say something stupid and thus appear ridiculous). The Venerable Somsak, chuckled and broke the silence, seemingly disappointed: “If you’re going to carry on like this, not saying a word, then I’m getting off at the next port.”

How to Entertain a Churel

Generally, a Churel is the spirit of a woman who died during childbirth, or otherwise died an unnatural death. Sometimes she can appear as a beautiful woman who seduces young people in order to steal their life-force; if a teenager were to run into her, his hair will prematurely turn white. Churels can be “sustained by their kin” (I’m not certain if Praveen meant they can “live at their kin’s expense” or that they literally “eat their family members,” but honestly it could be both). To get rid of a Churel or to prevent a dead person from becoming a Churel, you must observe the following rites:

  1. Prick the corpse’s thumbs or index fingers with nails.
  2. Bind the corpse’s toes together with an iron ring.
  3. Break the corpse’s legs above the ankle.
  4. Burn the corpse, upside down.
  5. Burn the corpse in a special location: for example, near the house in whose shade they used to escape from the hot, midday sunshine.
  6. Place a metal nail at the four corners of the burial site.
  7. Scatter mustard seeds along the route leading from the tomb to the deceased person’s old home.
  8. Scatter mustard seeds or thorns or iron nails on the threshold of the deceased person’s old home. It is believed that the spirit will be distracted by counting these small objects strewn on the ground.

(Likewise, the corpse is sometimes burned with a tangled skein of yarn or wool. It is believed that the woman’s spirit will be so occupied trying to untangle the skein that she’ll forget all about her quarrel with her family).

The Curse of the Koala

The Venerable Somsak tried using the Law of Karma to explain why the captain suffered from “the koala’s disease” (a new term he’d coined, eager as he was to avoid lending credence to the theory that a woman from Vanuatu had laid a curse upon the captain). The Fijians claimed that the women of Vanuatu were uglier than a Veli. They were toothless and old, so when they met a foreigner, they would place the leaf of a certain plant on the roof of their mouth and begin to speak to the stranger. This was the apparent explanation for why there were so many wealthy, blond, blue-eyed Australians, Englishmen, and Frenchmen who married women from Vanuatu.

“Let’s say that every action has an equal reaction,” began the speech of the Venerable Somsak. “All the people we now know, we also knew in our past lives; for example, in this life those who are now our parents were once our children in a previous life. So the captain, in a past life, met that woman, who treated him very well, but he didn’t treat her well; he hit her and starved her. It was like a marriage between an angel (her) and an ogre (him). Now, he must accept his karma, and carry it in his body forever in the form of the koala’s disease.

“That’s also why, for example, people who once wrote accounts or made drawings of temples or sacred places now have tattoos. They’re paying back the karma on their own body.” At this the Venerable Somsak glances at the ship’s petty officer to see if he’s offended, since the only blank spaces left on his body are his eyes. His teeth – or what few teeth he has left – are riddled with cavities and his tongue has been cut out. “I think the captain not only treated a woman poorly in a past life, but that he probably has already been a captain, and kept more than one lover in each port he visited. Men like that – who, in their past lives, have had many women at once and loved each of them without staying with any of them – in the depths of their souls these men desired to become a woman, so in their current incarnation they become transvestites, to pay back their karmic debt. Now they’re the ones who suffer because of men that don’t love them, and they can never fully satisfy their desire to become women either.”

The petty officer, a six-foot tall Fijian, corpulent and with stubbly hair, lowered his gaze, seemingly lost in thought. He began to rotate his right foot, moving his heel from side to side, as if crushing an imaginary bug beneath his big toe. Despite his skin made black from so many tattoos, his cheeks flushed red with blood. We realized that what we’d witnessed in the petty officer’s cabin was no Kabuki theatre performance. It was no act when, in Captain Ishikawa’s somnambulant wanderings, he dressed like a geisha and walked the halls while he elegantly waved his colorfully painted fan.

The Churel

Praveen Kumar Sharma was the great-great-great grandson, on his mother’s side, of a woman named Engi. His great-great-great grandmother had the ability to exorcise spirits, which was a very common practice in India. One night, when she was young, she went out for a walk in the jungle. She brought food and some tools, but had no lamp, and walking proved difficult in the dark. The only sounds were branches snapping and plants rustling, along with the occasional noises of nocturnal animals. That is until a voice said, “Engi, I’m hungry. I want to eat.”

It sounded like the pleading of a lost child. “Don’t worry, I’m here,” Engi answered.

At first, in the dark, she couldn’t make out who had asked her for food, so she offered them a loaf of bread and some sweets. When the figure stepped out of the shadows, however, the light of the moon illuminated her. Engi realized that the voice didn’t belong to a child, as she’d believed, but rather to a woman with long hair, fangs, and sharply filed nails. She was dressed in all white and her feet were backwards. Engi quickly shackled her by the ankle. “What have you done?” the Churel screamed. “Now I can’t return home!” Engi then claimed the spirit as her servant, and the Churel spent many years working for the family: feeding the cows, going to the market, all without complaint or payment for fourteen years. This continued until the day of the wedding.

The bride arrived at the house, and went to sit in the garden, where the Churel found her. She asked the bride to remove the shackle on her foot. On hearing this, and before the girl had a chance to remove the irons, Engi took an axe and hastily chopped off the Churel’s head.

If Praveen’s great-great-great grandmother had waited even a second longer, everyone in the wedding party would have been instantly killed (though we all secretly believed that the real reason Engi killed the Churel was out of fear of a lawsuit about poor working conditions for so many years).

How to Catch a Veli

“The people of Beqa, one of the Fijian islands,” declared Alisi Rabukawaga, “have special powers, like the ability to walk on hot stones. It’s not like in Hinduism, where they leap over hot coals. No, the people of Beqa walk on stones pulled directly from an active volcano. They possessed the ability to remove the heat from objects so that they don’t burn them.

“There was once a young man walking through the jungle who stopped to fish in a river. He didn’t notice at first that he’d caught an eel. When he retrieved his fishing net, the eel escaped, slipping away among the nearby rocks. The man pulled the rocks aside to retrieve the animal, and instead encountered a ‘Veli’ (which was strange because such spirits normally only inhabit mountainous areas in Fiji). ‘Please leave me alone. Don’t kill me!’ the spirit begged.

“‘But I trapped you,’ he replied.

“Then the Veli made a bargain: ‘if you release me, I’ll grant you a wish.’

“The man knew that this was the opportunity of a lifetime. A Veli is nearly impossible to catch, but if you manage it, they can make all your dreams come true. ‘Okay. I’ll release you, but I want you to give me the power to control heat.’ The Veli granted his wish. From then on, it appeared that the man could walk on and touch hot things without burning himself. In reality, whenever there was something hot enough to burn, the Veli would come and steal the object’s energy. So what walked on the volcanic rocks wasn’t, in fact, the man, but rather the Veli, placing itself as a buffer between the sole of his foot and the burning stones.

“But spirits can be very mischievous. Once a man trapped a Veli, and in exchange for its release, the spirit promised to give him all the food he could desire the very next day. The man agreed and he did indeed receive all the food he could desire, but only at his funeral. You see, he died the next day, and the neighbors and village people brought lots of food to his house to offer their condolences to his family. When it comes to a Veli, you can get what you want, but not always in the way you want, so you have to ask in a very smart and clear manner so the Veli doesn’t trick you. They’re like children; sometimes you find them in the house, throwing plates, opening drawers, and making lots of noise. If that happens, you should run because they’re very mischievous and will delight in openly mocking you.

“Velis are ugly, and they have a particular weakness for drinking Yaqona.[1] If you want to have someone killed, you can leave some Yaqona on the porch in exchange for the favor. But it’s not always effective. For example, there was a woman who was very jealous of my grandmother. One night, my grandmother was in the kitchen, and heard what sounded like someone running around the outside of the house. Shortly thereafter, there was a knock at the door. My grandmother opened it to reveal a Veli, who swayed slightly as it told her, ‘they’ve given me so much Yaqona to hurt you, that I’m actually sick of it.’ And anyway, because my grandmother prayed and was protected by the octopus and the shark, the Veli couldn’t do anything to her in the end.”

The Fruits of One’s Labor

When it became clear that we couldn’t shut him up, we decided that the best thing, for the safety of everyone on board the ship, was that Praveen tell the captain stories to keep him awake. In the end this didn’t work because the captain, with or without stories, would start snoring so loudly that eventually nobody else on board could get any sleep. Praveen carried on talking without pause anyway.

Then, even though we weren’t technically his crew, merely some passengers on a chartered commercial cruise, we decided to mutiny and lock the captain in his cabin. This didn’t work either, and he soon escaped: we watched him meander in his sleep, often after donning one of his beautiful kimonos, especially when it was a full moon and high tide. Still asleep, he sang a song in English that went, “I love you, my Little, Little-Jump,” and lapsed into reciting his dreams out loud, while they occurred. One day, he spoke of how the Nazis pursued him. In another dream it was the end of the world and he called his father with a cell phone. In a third, he told us about how he fucked Diego Maradona. His eyes were closed, but even if he opened them it didn’t make much difference, his Japanese heritage manifesting in the almond-shaped slits of his eyes.

Then Praveen told a story that his grandmother had often told him. She said there was once a fabulously wealthy king who had a very handsome son. Nothing could compete with this boy’s beauty. The king made an announcement throughout his lands; he said that the person who could show him a handsomer boy than his son would be given a coffer full of gold jewels and coins. A woman from a nearby farm came and presented her offspring to the king: in addition to behaving like an ape in front the assembly, the boy was thin, ragged, filthy, half-starved, toothless, cross-eyed, and lame. The king’s counsellors grasped the metaphor; that beauty is on the inside. That, to a mother, any child is perfect. They tried to explain this to the king. But he thought it was just a sick joke, that the woman wanted to take advantage of him. He ordered that the mother and her son be put to death.

Praveen finished his story only to be met by our stunned silence. He proceeded to try to explain it with metaphors: “this is like the story of the crow that was thirsty and couldn’t get water from the well. First there’s a theory, then an experiment, then the fruits of one’s labor. The crow couldn’t reach the water in the well with his beak, and there wasn’t much water in there anyway. He took a grain of sand and dropped it into the water. Then he took several grains of sand and dropped them in the well, too, but instead of raising the water level, the well just ended up drying out.” When he observed us still gazing in stunned silence at one another, he announced, “okay, I’m going to bed.” But then the silence became oppressive, and even the captain refrained from snoring or talking in his sleep. The first mate, whom we had assumed was mute, having lost his tongue in the Mariana Islands, decided to tell a story.

But it turned out we were right; he was mute, so he told us the story in sign language, and nobody understood a single word.

On How Praveen’s Great-great Grandmother Achieved Samadhi

Praveen’s great-great grandmother on his father’s side learned black magic in Bengal, because it was the only place one could learn such arts. She gained the power to transform herself into almost anything.

There was to be  a wedding, and Praveen’s great-great grandmother had been assigned the task of accompanying the bride to the house where the nuptial ceremony would be held. Back then in India, the journeys would last for at least three days, and the wedding for seven, because of the large distance to travel and the extensive ceremonies required.

The bride was aware that Praveen’s great-great grandmother had practiced black magic, and she asked for a spell to turn herself into a wolf:  “Please teach me how to transform myself.” Met with refusal, the bride threatened Praveen’s great-great grandmother: “I’m not going to move from this spot until you show me how to do it!”

Praveen’s great-great grandmother told her she couldn’t show her; they were very advanced spells, but what she could do was turn herself into a wolf. First, the girl had to climb to the top of a tree and try not to be frightened: “I’ll return to my human form again if you spray a bit of water on my head. If you see that I don’t react, please pour more water because if you don’t, there’s a risk I might eat you.”

The bride climbed the tree, and the great-great grandmother transformed herself into a hungry wolf. The girl was quite frightened and began to desperately pour water from above, but in doing so, she slipped and fell: the wolf promptly ate her. A half-hour later, Praveen’s great-great grandmother turned back into a human and, horrified by the crime she had just committed, sat down beneath the tree and entered Samadhi. That is to say, she reached a higher mental state of profound concentration, where neither anger, nor want, nor any other emotion could reach her. She never returned home again. 

The Liberation of the Fish

On instructions from the Venerable Somsak Piyaseelo, the sailors freed the live fish they had brough from Naha, releasing them on the high seas. They had been kept in fish tanks before being saved from their fate as part of the dinner menu prepared by the Filipino cooks each night.

The boat slowed slightly, and they freed the fish into the open ocean. The fish reclaimed their liberty, some swimming in circles, as if in thanks, before darting off into the infinite waves.

The Venerable Somsak had told all the sailors that their frequent nose bleeds were caused by their having killed so many fish to eat during their past lives: the blood flow represented that prior bloodshed, and they had to pay back the karmic debt by freeing fish into rivers or the sea, saving them from being sacrificed, too.

As we watched the liberation of the fish, we felt a great nostalgia and courage: we would no longer dine on seafood. We also understood why sailors used to keep talkative parrots on their shoulders: the sea was so unreal, was such an enormous impossibility, that they would otherwise hear Sirens and succumb to their songs. The sea could drive anyone insane, in that vast infinity, the middle of nowhere, so blue and interminable: a green, churning mass of dreams. We also knew that sailors had eventually foregone their chatterbox pets in exchange for a white powder that they obtained from the Solomon Islands, and which they snorted to avoid facing reality. That’s why their noses bled. Not because of any karmic debt.

The Tooth

Kubwimana Venuste’s mother was Tutsi; but she didn’t perish during the Rwandan genocide. Her death was due to complications from a head wound caused by shrapnel from a grenade that exploded near her. She died on Christmas day, 1999. Venuste was very attached to his mother, “Always follow your mind,” she told him, “and above all when it comes to forming emotional attachments.” In her eagerness to educate him, she told him many stories, one of which came from China. Venuste didn’t understand this particular story until many years later when he had the misfortune to fall in love with a Mexican woman who broke his heart. The girl was a volunteer at the Christian organization where Venuste worked (for no pay).

The story was about a rich man who went on a long journey. He arrived at his destination with all the money he had and decided to visit a brothel and have some fun. There, he met a woman with whom he fell madly in love. But money doesn’t last forever, so the man told the woman he had to return to his village to sell all his possessions before he could come back and live with her. The woman cried, she begged him not go, she confessed to him that he was the love of her life. She adored him. She’d never loved anyone else as much.

Eventually, when he did run out of money, the woman let him go, but first she said, “I’m afraid you’ll have some accident on the way, or you’ll decide not to return to my side. Leave one of your teeth here! At least that way, if you don’t return, I’ll have something of yours to hold onto, and when I’m feeling nostalgic, sad, and alone, I can look at it and remember you for as long as I live.” The man knew how painful it would be to pull out his tooth, but he did it to please her. He returned to his village to sell all he had in exchange for the funds he needed to return to his beloved.

Some time later, when he made it back, the woman’s neighbors recognized him, and started whispering behind his back. Some openly asked him, “what did you come back for? You know how things are with her.”

He made his way to the woman’s house. She received him coolly. He heard another male voice and asked her who was there. She told him, “the man I just slept with. I’m a prostitute, don’t you remember? Any man who pays can go to bed with me, and I have to take care of him.”

“But you told me you loved me. You asked for my tooth and I gave it to you,” the man managed, on the verge of crying in frustration.

“That’s a tradition of mine. I ask every man that sleeps with me for a keepsake. It’s not love. Affection doesn’t even come into it,” the woman responded.

“If you don’t love me, then give me back my tooth,” he demanded. The woman told him he could come in and look for it himself; he’d find it in a trunk out back.

When the man went out to look, he discovered that there were dozens of teeth mixed together in the trunk, alongside watches, rings, and other items taken from both men and women, and even, in some cases, animals. It was impossible to figure out which tooth was his.

The Vase and the Vixen

Santosh Giri told us a story that he said was widely known across Nepal. Even small children could recite it from memory. His grandfather had told it to him when he was six years old.

The story begins like this: there was once an old couple who lived out in the country. They were very poor, and always had to go into the forest to cut firewood or find something to eat. One day the old man left his wife to go out in search of sustenance. As he was trying to cut the branches of a tree to make  a fire to warm the house, he suddenly spotted a fox. The old man was afraid and climbed up into the tree. The fox, a vixen, addressed him: “Hey! You! Old man! I’m very hungry; I want to eat you! Let me eat you! Come down so I can eat you!”

“Oh fox, please don’t eat me,” the old man begged. “I have a poor, old wife at home. I’m only here only to chop a bit of firewood so we can cook something; we’re hungry, too. But I’ll give you something to eat if you come to my house.”

The fox didn’t believe him. “I want to eat you now,” she insisted.

“No, wait!” the old man pleaded. “We can dine together at my house tonight. Please, go away now and I’ll be able to give you something to eat there.”

“Fine then. I’ll be at your house at five this evening. Make sure you prepare enough food for me to eat, too.”

“Oh fox, thank you so much.” With that the fox ran off, leaving him alone.

When the old man got home, he told his wife what had happened to him. She thought it unlikely that her husband (a skinny, wrinkled old man) could have climbed a tree while a fox discussed how she wanted to eat him. She thought this was perhaps an early sign of senile dementia. There was, as usual, very little food and they were, as usual, very hungry, so when they finished eating, nothing was left. Still, they didn’t really believe the fox would come to the house to call in the bargain until five that evening, when they heard a knock at the door. The couple panicked and hid inside an enormous, clay vase.

“Hello? Hello? Is anyone home? I’ve come for the food I was promised,” the fox said. When she opened the door and found nothing but the empty plates on the table, she began to growl. The old couple, trembling with fear inside the vase, remained silent, until the old woman said, very quietly so the fox wouldn’t hear them, “I can’t take it anymore; I have to fart. What do I do?”

He husband whispered, “well fart if you have to, as long as it’s quiet, so the fox doesn’t hear us because then she’ll figure out that we’re hiding here.” The old woman quietly passed gas and, even in the enclosed space, the fumes were bearable. But then the spices from their meal started to take effect in the old man’s stomach, and eventually he could no longer contain the sound. He released a thunderous fart; the reverberation shattered the vase, causing pieces of clay to go flying through the air. This so frightened the fox that she turned and fled from the place.

The Legend of the Cursed Toilet

When Fernando Euripides Iglesias heard Praveen’s story about the Churel, he said that was nothing compared to a story he remembered, told throughout Brazil. It was a classic tale featuring a beautiful, twenty-year-old blond girl, who, for some unknown reason, was still in middle school (though the rest of her classmates had generally graduated at fifteen).

The girl had gotten pregnant, and when she told the baby’s father, another teenager as irresponsible and attractive as she, he got angry, and screamed that she was a whore. The girl went to the school bathroom, never to leave it again: she had decided to kill herself as dramatically as possible (we don’t know if she tried to drink the toilet water little by little and died after a while from vomiting it all up, or if her death was simply the result of a terrible typhoid fever compounded by sudden-onset hepatitis contracted from drinking water laced with excrement, which, if we’re being realistic, is the only appropriate way to die in such a place). What we do know is that people started to hear grunts and terrible cries from the bathroom, and they were terrified of entering because surely the beautiful blonde’s ghost was still there. Either that, or it was someone having horrible diarrhea.

Now no one can enter that bathroom without fear, and rightly so (though they should be most frightened of running out of toilet paper or contracting some venereal disease from sitting on the infrequently washed bowls).

The Hunter’s Wife, The Butcher’s Sister

Saif Al Kindi told us that, in a town in Oman, the local butcher’s sister married a hunter. The couple moved to the forest so that he could hunt from sunup to sundown. Every night, he would come back fuming, and ask his wife to lie back while he placed a hoop on her stomach for target practice. Then he would shoot an arrow that passed through the middle of the ring without touching it. He wanted to demonstrate his good aim. His wife was terrified the whole time, but she didn’t say anything because she didn’t want him to be angered further.

After several months, the butcher visited his sister. When he arrived, he was startled to see that she had gown thin and seemed ill and listless. He demanded to know what had happened to alter her so. Was her husband not feeding her? Did he treat her poorly? She was reluctant to talk at first but gave in to brother’s insistence and told him everything. He grumbled, “I’m going to teach that man a lesson.”

At night the hunter returned to find that his brother-in-law was visiting. The hunter had very long hair; as was always in the forest, there was nobody to cut it for him. So the butcher offered to give him a haircut, and the hunter gladly accepted. The butcher asked him to lie down. He took up his axe, sharpened it, positioned the hunter’s head, lifted the axe, and let it fall, cutting off the hunter’s hair almost to the scalp. The hunter didn’t even dare to flinch. Not satisfied yet, the butcher grabbed one of the sharpened knives with which he skinned animals and, placing it against the hunter’s neck, began to shave his beard.

When the butcher had finished, he asked the hunter, “did you enjoy your shave and haircut?” The hunter remained silent for a few minutes. The butcher repeated the question.

The hunter released a sigh of relief, and finally said, “the haircut looks good, but I’m certain the fright you gave me added 20 years to my age. What little hair you left me is probably gray now.”

To which the butcher responded, “and that was just one haircut. Imagine if you came home from hunting every night and shot an arrow over my sister’s stomach to practice your aim. How many years would you have added to her age?  How many gray hairs would you have given her?” The hunter promised never to treat his wife like that again.

Landbound or airborne

Everyone on board knew that the sultanate of Oman was renowned for its sorcerers. We’d heard about the dangers of wandering alone through the desert, especially when darkness begins to fall: if a sorcerer catches you, they’ll take possession of your mind and turn you into their slave. That’s why Omanis can suddenly show up in India, without no recollection of how they got there.

We were beginning to suspect that it wasn’t “the curse of the koala” that had affected the captain. That it was actually Saif Al Kindi, who was one of those sorcerers who enslaved people’s minds. In order to avoid our accusations, Saif told us a story about how there was once a ruler in Oman, many years ago, who was a compulsive liar. He used to tell impossible stories every night. He had a minister whose job it was to legitimize the stories, so that the people would believe the ruler. Because of this, the minister had earned the nicknamed “The Fixer.”

But one day the minister died, and the sultan no longer wanted to appear before his people to tell his stories, because nobody would believe him without someone to substantiate his claims. For several years, he refused to appear before his subjects at all. His secretary, worried to see the sultan so glum, asked him what the problem was, and the sultan responded, “I’m still mourning my dear minister’s death.”

“Don’t try to fool me like you do everyone else; I’m your closest friend, and I know you’re lying,” replied the secretary.

So the sultan confessed, “I’m sad because I miss how the minister used to twist my lies and make them believable. I can’t tell impossible stories anymore.”

The secretary pointed out that the people who used to come listen to his stories didn’t attend alone. Normally, they were accompanied by their family: brothers, sons, cousins, etc. So he set out in search of someone among all those families who, with some luck, might be able to help.

After searching for a long time, the secretary found that the minister himself had two sons, 25 and 21 years old, and he brought them before the sultan. On meeting them, the sovereign said, “I have a question for each of you. What’s bigger: a mouse,” (making a gesture with his arms as if measuring an invisible mouse that was at least a yard in length), “or a donkey?” (spreading his thumb and index finger on one hand, as if to signal a donkey as small as a button). The older brother said, “even if I were blind, and couldn’t see your hands, I’d still know that a donkey is much bigger than a mouse.”

The king said, “you’re useless to me.”

Now it was the younger brother’s turn, and the sultan asked him the same question, to which he responded, “of course, oh magnanimous Sultan. If you say that the mouse is that size, then there can be no doubt that it’s larger than the donkey.”

The sultan was happy: “You’re the one I’ve been searching for.”

The next morning, the sultan gathered all his subjects together for an audience. After hearing the people’s petitions, the attendees had one last request: “Oh, beloved Sultan! We miss your stories, the ones you used to tell when your minister was still alive.”

“Ah, my dear minister. How I miss him!” and then the sultan launched into his tale: “I was just thinking of him this morning as I was wandering through the fields, when I happened upon a dead horse, but instead of maggots filling its rotting carcass, the body was swarming with kittens.”

The people exclaimed in alarm: “Kittens? But that’s impossible! Decomposing flesh could attract cockroaches or even mice, but kittens? Impossible!”

The new minister quickly stepped in: “Our beloved sultan speaks the truth. He saw kittens in the dead animal. You’ll be aware that we’ve had several rainy and cold days recently. The mother cat might have hidden her litter in the horse’s body to keep them warm. Perhaps when the cat left them to go out in search of food, the kittens, still not even old enough to open their eyes, climbed out of the horse’s body, crying for their mother.”

The people were satisfied with this explanation. But the sultan wasn’t finished yet: “once I went out for a morning walk, and I saw dogs barking in the sky.”

The audience cried in confusion: “How? In the sky? Dogs can’t fly, that’s preposterous.”

But the fixer was ready to support his sultan: “Our beloved monarch speaks the truth.”

Still, the people persisted in their disbelief: “It’s not true! How can it be possible? Has anyone ever seen a flying dog?”

The new minister calmly continued, “perhaps some kind of big marine mammal came up onto the beach to die. Dogs could be attracted by the smell of the rotting animal, and come to feast on the beast’s carrion, a beached whale for example. They climbed into the whale through its stomach and head and remained inside its corpse all night. In the morning, eagles and vultures spotted the whale’s body from above, and swooping down onto it, flew off towards their nests in the mountain, clutching pieces of the whale in their powerful talons. The hidden dogs had been caught up along with the whale’s flesh. On finding themselves at a great height, the vertigo and the fear of flying caused them to bark, which the sultan heard from above.”

The still incredulous people shot back, “but the sultan just said he’d seen dogs barking in the sky, not that he’d heard them. That’s enough lies. We’re going home before you tell another ridiculous fiction.”

The sultan turned to the fixer: “I like you for this job.”

But the minister said, “forgive me your highness, but I don’t agree with what you said today.”

The sultan was indignant: “But why? Everything went well.”

The minister forged ahead, “you know, when you lie it’s best for the stories to keep their feet on the ground. It’s easier to plant lies that way, but if you launch them into the sky, it’s harder to do so. Today you mentioned that a dog flew, and dogs are pretty light, but tomorrow you might say that an elephant flies, and that’s much heavier to keep aloft.”

Saif concluded, “so from that day forward, lies in Oman are classified in one of two ways: landbound lies that people can more easily believe or the airborne ones, which are harder to sustain.”

We all applauded Saif enthusiastically, mostly because we’d finally understood someone’s story. The whole crew had gathered around him. Even the captain, who was awake for once, though he went right back to sleep afterwards. That’s when someone asked, “if the whole crew is here, even the Filipino cooks, who’s steering this ship?”

美高 (Great Beauty)

The communist regime during 美高 (Mei Gao)’s youth wasn’t anything to write home about. She told us that, if the situation called for it, even one’s own spouse might denounce them to the authorities, and land them in jail for life: “you couldn’t trust anybody back then.”

Despite her advanced age – we guessed that she was over 70 – we were obliged to stop the boat so she could dive into the sea and swim every morning at dawn (in her youth she’d been on the Chinese national swim team and was only prevented from competing in the Olympics by a back injury sustained during training). She was the companion of the old, German invalid, who, despite being an author, never spoke, preferring to listen attentively to the stories we told at night when nobody on board could sleep because of the loud snoring of Captain Ishikawa (the one who suffered from the koala’s disease).

Mei was a woman of strong character; after the events of Tiananmen Square, she fled from the German Democratic Republic hidden in a fish tank and sought political asylum in the Federal Republic of Germany. The Chinese government had sent her to Humboldt University of Berlin to specialize in German microfiction, and to “do some research,” as she termed it.

We were pretty sure that she’d actually been a spy for the Chinese government during the Cold War. She’d been a member of the Chinese Red Guards. She proudly described the marches she attended with Mao as a girl and that she’d had the privilege of seeing the great leader greet his people from the balcony of Tiananmen Gate. She also told us of how she cried at his passing; the whole country wailed themselves into hysteria. She spoke, too, of how people committed suicide in the factories, throwing themselves off the highest towers: “You couldn’t trust anybody,” she repeated. She recalled the great Chinese famine: “We had to eat potatoes and horse meat because there was nothing else. It was a noble sacrifice.” We glanced at each other because for many of us, that was a perfectly normal diet in our countries or aboard the ship, but it seemed that the Chinese weren’t used to it.

One day, one of her companions dropped a lapel pin that depicted Mao on its head. The pin rolled on the ground; in her eagerness to stop the small, round piece of metal and keep it from being lost in the machinery, she did the easiest thing, which was to step on it. Stomping on the image of the great leader, however, warranted a punishment. She was made to extend her arms overhead with her knees bent, as if preparing to dive into a pool. She had to hold this position for three days without bathroom or food breaks. What’s more, she had to spend several months in a reeducation camp.

The Map

Yap, Chuuk, Pohnpei, and Kosrae are the four main states of Micronesia. Franky Kustin told us that on his island of Pohnpei – the capital, whose name meant “upon a stone altar” – eels would fall from the sky along with the rain. The people of the island didn’t know why this happened. Franky’s clan loved the eels, because they knew that if one were to eat an eel, they could die.

He advised us to take care when yelling in the jungle, especially if we were accompanied by another person. If you yell the other person’s name, “a being” might answer, and carry you off, erase your memories and thoughts, and leave your mind a completely blank slate. To avoid that fate, what you need to do is get the other person’s attention with a “hey,” or whistle, but never call them by their name out loud.

Likewise, we shouldn’t fall asleep near a river, because rivers belong to witches. When you wake up from your slumber, your mind will be backwards. For example, you could be naked and not realize it or think you’re seeing a beautiful woman, but there’s not really anyone there. The greatest danger is at midnight, when you might hear a voice at your back and, upon turning around, realize you’re completely alone.

Throughout its history, the island has been home to practitioners of black magic. For example, if someone raced competitively, but was rather slow and likely to lose, he would ask his family to gather all the leaves from the trees and perform spells over them. The athletic aspirant would eat the leaves or rub them over his entire body, and he would then run faster. Franky’s cousin even had a spell book, but he was unwilling to share it with anyone because if he showed it to too many people, it would lose its potency. That’s why he kept it a secret and it only passed from generation to generation, father to son.

There’s a well-known archeological site in PonhPei built entirely of stone. Years ago, people would see spirits in the photos that tourists took there. There was a path at the site that led to the ocean, but it began in the mountains, where there grew a tree that could only be seen by helicopter; if you attempted to view it from the ground, it would disappear.

One year, a Japanese businessman went to expand the airport, but the land needed belonged to two women who were witches. The project was scheduled to be completed in three months, so the Japanese man couldn’t understand why it had been delayed for nearly three years. That is until people explained it was because he had felled trees belonging to the women. Since then, construction had stalled, and it seemed like things were only getting worse. They excavated mud and laid down bedrock, but the rocks immediately sank after being placed, disappearing to no one knew where; it seemed as if the earth was a bottomless pit of mud. As for the two women; they would go walking through the village at night, and once every two weeks they would pass through the outskirts to see whether or not the airport construction was progressing. The locals knew whenever they passed through because the neighborhood dogs would bark all night long, only stopping once the women had disappeared.

The very act of mapping PonhPei implied a lack of respect for the island; the spirits didn’t like it. An American once asked the locals to tattoo the map of PonhPei on his arm. Shortly before he was going to leave, he was in the market buying some food when, out of nowhere, someone shot him. They never figured out who his attacker was; they hadn’t stolen anything. The same thing happened to an Austrian; he asked someone to tattoo a map of the island on his back. The next airplane ride he took was his last. Franky said that’s why it’s forbidden to write down any stories from the island, because you could die or go permanently insane. You had to go and see PohnPei, but never, ever write about it.

If someone did dare to write a story about the island, the only way the writer could free themselves from the curse would be to transmit it to their reader. When the reader automatically arrives at the final period, their misfortunes will begin.

The Nine Orifices

The Venerable Somsak said that in Thailand they believed that if the food you were eating suddenly fell to the ground, it was because of the spirits of impish children, who slapped it out of your hands just because they felt like it. But we knew it was really because the boat was moving so much.

The tide rose and the waves crashed against the ship, causing us to jump. We could do little else but vomit up any food we managed to bring to our mouths without dropping it. When the boat tilted to the right, we shuffled towards the right, but soon the boat shifted to the left, and we shuffled left just as the boat swayed right again, rocking us from side to side.

Then the Venerable Somsak, imperturbable in his half lotus pose, smiling with his eyes half closed, the only one of us not moving, offered us a parable. “The Teacher of Teachers, The Sage of Sages said that for one to sit well and be healthy, they need their eight orifices to be balanced, you see, so that they aren’t drained. If one of them is off-balance, there is no health.” We kept rocking from side to side on the ship and along with the rising tide we saw the Venerable Somsak begin to levitate. “If you find yourself in a friend’s company, it is courteous to ask, ‘how are your two eyes, how are your two nostrils, how is your mouth, how are your two ears?’” Here the Venerable Somsak paused.

“That’s only seven holes!” shouted a sailor, eager to demonstrate his prowess in mathematics.

“Well of course, I’ve omitted the most important orifice of all:

Adrift on a Sleepless Sea

Carmen Ávila

Translated from Spanish into English by Olivia Caputo

Awarded the 2013 Rafael Ramírez Heredia National Story Prize by the Tampico Tamaulipas Council in Mexico.

Prologue

As a reader, I sometimes prefer to wait until the end of a book to read the prologue. I’m always worried it will spoil something important in the story. But now that I’m the one writing the prologue, I’m not remotely tempted to quote paragraphs from the book, or even to give you a general synopsis, merely an appetizer.

Adrift on a Sleepless Sea is divided, or united, as you’ll see, into three parts. In each of these parts, Carmen proves her narrative chops with her irreverent style. She’s a writer who takes risks with her different approaches, and the results are well worth it. She constructs for us a cathedral of absurdism, surrealism, and magical realism. She interweaves the detective novel with the anecdote and the fable into the shape of a lively narrative, unburdened by excesses, and infused with the sense of humor and naturalness that are her greatest weapons.

Carmen has created an atemporal dreamland that bends the laws of physics, where characters come and go, drifting through her stories. Adrift on a Sleepless Sea contains the reincarnated spirit of a twisted Scheherazade who climbed into bed with the Brothers Grimm as well as something of an exotic Robert Louis Stevenson that dreamed he was a butterfly that dreamed he was an exotic Robert Louis Stevenson.

Carmen Ávila’s narrative has one other specific advantage, which is discernible from the very first page: she is an excellent poet. It would suffice just to say she’s a poet, but to my mind she’s an excellent one. As a writer who is also able to navigate the waters of poetry, she can draw from wider range of resources to conjure the images she floats our way, rendering them very real in our minds, for all their unreality. 

The stories in this book are built upon the author’s influences, obsessions, and memories. She’ll explain them in her own time within these pages, so I see no reason to repeat them here. I will mention, however, that if Carmen were to spin a globe and stop it by pointing at random, it would not come as a shock if her finger landed on a place that she has been before. This is due to her insatiable curiosity to know other cultures, places, and people. When she sent me the manuscript for Adrift on a Sleepless Sea with an invitation to write this prologue, she gave as a reason (in addition to our shared affinity for literature and the friendship that unites us), our love of traveling the world. To be fair, there are some glaring differences between us; Carmen is less selfish when she travels (though all travelers are inherently somewhat selfish, which is not necessarily a bad thing). Carmen manages to get involved in humanitarian causes, undertake academic studies, participate in conferences, get under people’s skin, live simply when necessary, learn languages. All of this has made her more sensitive and empathetic, which leads to a more comprehensive understanding of those faraway places, an understanding for which she is repaid when the time comes to create her literary postcards.

To travel is to leave one’s comfort zone, to revert to being a child; a being that lacks the words to express oneself, for whom new foods are unfamiliar. To travel is to once again experience that forgotten fear and uncertainty. Carmen is an example of that perpetual return to childhood and its stories: in this case they are the myths and legends of the other adventurers we meet along the way.

Incidentally, her book reminds me of a phrase I once heard, by the songwriter (and avid traveler) Facundo Cabral, which goes something like this: “If the world is round, then I don’t know what it means to go forward.” Likewise, Carmen’s ship roams without a compass, guided by the swells and storms that bring in its stories. But I’ll stop myself here, so as not to give away any of those details that will ruin the surprise. 

Finally, I want to thank my dear, admired Carmen Ávila for allowing me to be a part of this book; I know how important it is to her. So without further delay, I invite you to board this rudderless ship of stories and undertake, as I have had the privilege to do, a marvelous journey through the author’s sleepless sea.

Miguel Barquiarena

The Shortest Story in All German Literature (1945)

(Siegfried Wolf, 1945)

On the Danube

A slightly insane, foreign collector placed an internet ad selling, for 20 euros, the historical remnants of the “Oberdonau Zeitung,” the premiere newspaper from the province in which Hitler was born. It primarily served to inform workers at the factory and nearby concentration camp of bad tidings from the war.

The newspaper had dwindled to only two sheets as the war dragged on. The second to last edition, from the 26th of April, 1945, declares that American tanks are entering the town and Russian soldiers (the Soviet army) have reached the forests on the outskirts of Vienna. Classified ads inquire about missing persons, in hopes of finding them alive, and about private property that was destroyed by the bombings.

Roberto Bolaño claimed that the final issue of “Oberdonau Zeitung” was printed on the 27th of April, 1945. It consisted of a single page, on which was published a famous story by German author Siegfried Wolf: the shortest story in all German literature.

The Hand of God and the Foot of D10S

Diego “El Pelusa” Maradona was always telling me about that blessed day, the 22nd of July 1986, when Argentina beat England in the world cup quarterfinals. They had decided to hold the match in Monterrey because they knew that the southern heat and humidity would kill the Germans. The Shortest Story in All the World

Luis Gómez was my German lit teacher. He liked to brag about having written the shortest story in all the world. It was a single piece of paper on which one could read the following:

Now it would be easy to look at that and assume that a fly had taken a shit precisely on the center of the page, but no. His story was a period: a simple, solitary period. When it was published in the country’s most prestigious and serious literary magazine (which, I should mention, was a real feat as he had never published anything else and only managed this because the editor owed him a favor), it produced a chain reaction: all manner of accolades flooded the newspapers, independent reviews, and literary internet forums. But so, too, did a wave of sarcastic, biting criticism.

His detractors claimed that he was mocking his reader. They complained bitterly: “We live in an age where there is no respect nor ethics in the arts, and we see literature being written, more and more, by those utterly lacking in scruples.” His dearest friends showered him with flowers and flattery. They took it upon themselves to hunt down metaphors: “With this period, the writer symbolizes the end of literature: after this, nobody can write anything new anymore; it’s all been said, there’s nothing more to add.” 

Thanks to the story’s publication, Luis Gómez was offered countless honorary doctorates; he gave lectures in several universities in the United States, among them Stanford and Yale. But he also spoke at the most prestigious institutions in Europe like Oxford, Humboldt, Salamanca, La Sapienza, and the Sorbonne. Even though his specialty was technically German literature, the university decided to offer him a special professorship in “nanofiction,” as microfiction had already gone out of style: he himself had rendered it obsolete with his story.

Andrés was necessary to our plan because Bolaño wrote that the German author, Siegfried Wolf, had last been seen in Ecuador, somewhere in the Amazon rainforest. Jade and I hoped first to find Siegfried alive, and then to interview him, to at last decipher the enigma of his mysterious story.   

For that we needed someone capable of guiding us on a trek through the heart of the Ecuadorian Amazon to uncover Siegfried’s whereabouts. Somewhere in the Ecuadorian Rainforest

Wednesday, May 25, 7:00am

Several armed men arrive at the parish. Among them is the famous guerilla fighter Andrés Sarquis, renowned throughout the Amazon; his camp must be nearby. Everyone in the village knows how to get in touch with him. The villagers gather provisions for the rebels, who offer protection in exchange for the food the people give them: a lovely little guerilla ecosystem. We’re fascinated by how the vestiges of the largely extinct, rural guerrilla forces that Régis Debray devoted so much time and attention to – an interesting parallel since it’s his fault that Che Guevara was discovered hiding in Bolivia – remain active in the Ecuadorian wilderness.

Strange as it seems, I note a strong resemblance between our Andrés and General Andrés Sarquis. They’re almost carbon copies of each other, except that General Sarquis is graying and looks worn out from his time in the mountains. Our Andrés Yañez, 23 years Sarquis’s junior, is also worn out, but by alcohol, whoring, and the hunger he experienced in Mexico. I swear they look like father and son.

The general is cordial towards us, inviting us to visit their camp. Once there, we observe some combat-training maneuvers drawn up by the men. We get to see how they exercise and cook their food. They store just enough water to drink and shave, which means they bathe in a nearby stream. I swear that Sarquis and Andrés have the same facial expressions and even make the same, odd gestures. I think their tone of voice is almost identical, too. It’s honestly a surprise they don’t share a last name as well as the first.

Andrés fills the general in on our mission to find a famous writer who, after the Second World War, fled Germany to seek refuge in Ecuador. “If you plan to have dealings with that man, you’ll need to tread carefully. This Hairwolf guy is a crazy old Kraut,” he advised us. But we were confident that Siegfried Wolf wouldn’t deny us a simple interview. After all, we only wanted to decipher the enigma of his story published in Oberdonau in 1945 and, if there was time, ask if he’d kept a copy of it that he could sign for us.

At this point, the guerrillas started to carouse and drink. They ended up drinking enough to render them useless in any possible standoff we might have with Siegfried and his maid the following day.

Miniature Amazonian Neuschwanstein

Thursday, May 28, 4:00pm

The mountain is shrouded in a thick fog, but in the distance, you can make out Siegfried’s house. The structure looks very similar to Neuschwanstein, the Bavarian castle that Disney later copied for its parks. We arrive and knock on the outer gate’s wooden door, but nobody opens it. The doorbell is positioned so that you have to reach high up to ring it, and we realize that, without meaning to, we’re giving the photo a Nazi salute. This leads us to believe that Siegfried is a rather clever person, with a singular sense of humor.

Through the intercom a voice, old-sounding and with a thick German accent, demands to know we want. We respond that we want an interview, and he tells us to get lost with a “Verpiss dich,” which literally means “piss yourself,” but Jade and I take it as “váyanse a la chingada (go fuck yourself)” and Andrés translates it to the more Ecuadorian version, “tu chucha madre.” We beg him to speak to us; we’re merely literature students, we want to interview him because our thesis is about his work, his only story, published in the Oberdonau newspaper in 1945, considered the shortest story ever penned in the history of German literature. That’s when he opens the security gate to allow us pass into the garden.

To get from the garden entrance to the mansion door, we have to travel about six hundred feet. Across that distance, we see an old man in a wheelchair, petting his three German shepherds. He tells the dogs to “Steh” when they see us and ready themselves to run and attack us: they freeze at his command. The elderly man approaches in his electric wheelchair. The chair’s motor is fast. It only takes him a few seconds to arrive in front of us. He raises a hand to greet us. He’s wearing a brown shirt to which are pinned several war medals. Jade giggles and murmurs to me that his little mustache reminds her of Charlie Chaplin. I keep my thoughts to myself for now, but I get the feeling that we’ve uncovered something the world meant to keep buried.

Thursday, May 28, 4:24pm

Welcome to Little-Jump, (A.K.A. Saltillo)

(Some notes about me, my family, and my home)

Papa is a Doll Made of Old Rags

When the S.S. officials entered our house, I had no trouble recognizing them thanks to the swastikas on their sleeves. My father and I were in the central patio at the time. Before I climbed onto the roof to try to escape, my father handed me a photograph. “This is the key,” he told me. From my perch, I watched as the soldiers barged in and grabbed him by his shoulders. Hanging by his armpits as they dragged him away, Papa looked like a doll made of old rags and scraps. The toes of his dilapidated shoes scraped along the ground. The fingertips of his gloves had been eaten away by moths and he wore an old, Russian ushanka, and a dirty coat that was patched at the elbows. He offered no resistance as they carried him off, their military-issue helmets gleaming. I ran away along the roof so they wouldn’t see me. The photograph he’d given me was blurry, and no matter how long I looked at it, I couldn’t make out the image, only a mass of meaningless colors. Nor did I know what he meant by calling it “the key,” but I kept the photo anyway.

Jesus is Angry Because I Ate his Cloud

The sky was full of clouds that day; but it looked different somehow. When I looked up as I went out to the patio to play, the sky’s appearance made me run right back inside. I was hungry. So hungry, in fact, that I thought the clouds were made of cotton candy, which my mom bought for me whenever we went out. I had only to bite the pink and spongy sweet for it to hit my tongue and instantly melt in my mouth. I thought that eating a cloud would be a similar sensation. I went outside again and started throwing stones at the sky, trying to knock down a cloud to calm my aching belly. The first stone I threw didn’t reach the sky. I threw another with the same result: it fell right back down, landing at my feet. I threw the third, and this finally knocked down a piece of cloud to eat. I shoved the cloud into my waiting mouth, but, much to my disappointment, it just tasted like water. I looked up to find that the piece of cloud that I’d eaten had left a hole in the sky. Jesus Christ leaned out of the hole I’d made in the cloud. In the middle of his chest he had a small, flaming heart surrounded by thorns, as if it were a pin to hold up his robes. He didn’t say anything; he only glared at me. I knew that God was angry with me because I ate a piece of his property, and I felt very bad and ashamed. I felt so bad that I ran back into my grandmother’s house. When I dared a glance outside to see if Jesus had gone away, I saw that he was still there, remaining completely silent. But he kept looking at me in that ominous, angry way.

Ice Skating Prostitutes

The brothel I work at has an ice-skating rink. We serve food and drinks on skates, and all the girls have to put on those pretty costumes to make us look like real figure skaters. The stretchy material has the added benefit of allowing for flexibility. I go to my room to change into my costume. I’m not wearing any underwear, so I get completely undressed. I can hear grunts coming from next door. I can also hear them calling my name out on the floor, but I’m still not ready; I haven’t finished putting on my blue leotard to go out on the ice. My boss calls me in and tells me off, threatening violence: “how can you make the clients wait?” she demands. As she leaves, she orders me to see to the patrons. That’s when I finally decide, “never again. Never again,” and I get the hell out of there. I steal a client’s  motorcycle to make my escape. “Never again,” I repeat to myself, the cold air buffeting my shoulders as I drive down the lonely highway away from the city. I glance up at the night sky. The white and gray clouds on the horizon tell me that rain’s not far off.

Why I’m Single

I was at a party. Everyone was laughing because my boyfriend was sharing amusing anecdotes, but then he told one that was about me. I couldn’t hear him well, but everyone else laughed when he got to the end.
